    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5693987579175079846">Poema épico, se trata del texto más representativo del arte de los juglares españoles de la Edad Media. El poema fue probablemente compuesto entre 1110-1140, no mucho después de los hechos a los que se refiere. El poema, dividido en tres partes o cantares, narra el destierro y las aventuras del Cid, Rodrigo Díaz de Vivar. El tema del deshonor y la recuperación de la honra </span>
  <span id="freeText5693987579175079846" style="display:none">Poema épico, se trata del texto más representativo del arte de los juglares españoles de la Edad Media. El poema fue probablemente compuesto entre 1110-1140, no mucho después de los hechos a los que se refiere. El poema, dividido en tres partes o cantares, narra el destierro y las aventuras del Cid, Rodrigo Díaz de Vivar. El tema del deshonor y la recuperación de la honra constituyen el eje central de la obra, que describe la mentalidad y los valores éticos de la época. Muchos de los personajes y hechos que muestra están atestiguados, lo cual le confiere un gran valor histórico.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5693987579175079846" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2194253362" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2194253362" />
    <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin"><img alt="Bill Kerwin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442940975p2/83582.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2194253362?book_show_action=true">Nov 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Kerwin" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin">Bill Kerwin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780806130224" href="/book/show/261094.Poem_of_the_Cid">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=spanish-lit">spanish-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=epic">epic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2194253362" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6806076864002878599"><br/><i>The Poem of the Cid</i>, the first great work of Spanish literature, tells the story of the 11th century military leader Rodrigo Diaz de Vivar, his unjust exile, his rehabilitation through military conquest and tribute, and his search for justice following the humiliation and abuse of his two daughters at the hands of their husbands.<br/><br/>I wanted to like this better than I did. After all, it is the first known production of a great nation, the heroic expression of a people, and I had prepared myself to l</span>
  <span id="freeText6806076864002878599" style="display:none"><br /><i>The Poem of the Cid</i>, the first great work of Spanish literature, tells the story of the 11th century military leader Rodrigo Diaz de Vivar, his unjust exile, his rehabilitation through military conquest and tribute, and his search for justice following the humiliation and abuse of his two daughters at the hands of their husbands.<br /><br />I wanted to like this better than I did. After all, it is the first known production of a great nation, the heroic expression of a people, and I had prepared myself to love it and embrace as I do <i>The Iliad</i>, <i>Beowulf</i>, and <i>The Song of Roland.</i><br /><br />Perhaps the fault is with the translation. I chose Paul Blackburn because I have always admired his plain colloquial language and his spare imagery, and I like the fact that he produced a translation to be read aloud. But although there was dignity and poignancy here, there was no real majesty or epic darkness. Later, I picked up the W.S. Merwin for comparison. I found more dignity and more poignancy in Merwin’s verse, but less joy, less narrative purity. And the majesty and epic darkness eluded me here as well.<br /><br />I came to see <i>The Poem of the Cid</i> not so much as an epic, but as a series of something akin to three very long ballads, similar to the “Robin Hood Cycle” of English tradition: the first ballad is about the Cid’s exile and early victories, the second about his conquests and rehabilitation, the third about his search for justice and the restoration of honor.<br /><br />One I got used to <i>The Poem</i> not being an epic—at least according to my definition—I began to see that it possessed compensatory virtues. Since it dispenses with the supernatural machinery usually found in the epic, it concentrates instead on a realistic portrait of the time, an innocent delight in battle strategy, the bravery of warfare, the magnificence of treasure won on the field, the clear imperatives of honor.<br /><br />I recommend it. No, it’s not Homer. But it has down-to-earth enthusiasm and a narrative purity all its own.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6806076864002878599" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1402798355" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1402798355" />
    <a title="Roy Lotz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz"><img alt="Roy Lotz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443324405p2/14046996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1402798355?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roy Lotz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roy Lotz" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz">Roy Lotz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140444469" href="/book/show/261088.The_Poem_of_the_Cid">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=eurotrip">eurotrip</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=lets-get-medieval">lets-get-medieval</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=best-words-best-order">best-words-best-order</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1402798355" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1574163342369386570">This book has been sitting around my bookshelves for a long time, ever since a friend from college gave it to me on a whim; and because of my impending trip to Spain, I finally decided to pick it up. It is a quick and light read; and I was pleased to find out that this is the oldest extant epic poem in Castilian, and a foundational work of Spanish literature. So I’ve done my homework.<br/>		<br/>The poem tells the story of Rodrigo Díaz de Vivar (1043 – 1099), a medieval Spanish nobleman and military leade</span>
  <span id="freeText1574163342369386570" style="display:none">This book has been sitting around my bookshelves for a long time, ever since a friend from college gave it to me on a whim; and because of my impending trip to Spain, I finally decided to pick it up. It is a quick and light read; and I was pleased to find out that this is the oldest extant epic poem in Castilian, and a foundational work of Spanish literature. So I’ve done my homework.<br />		<br />The poem tells the story of Rodrigo Díaz de Vivar (1043 – 1099), a medieval Spanish nobleman and military leader of great skill. (By the way, the honorific title “Cid” is a Spanish loan word from the Arabic <i>sîdi</i>, meaning “lord.”) Perhaps unsurprisingly, the poem has mythologized El Campeador’s life considerably. (“Campeador” is another honorific for the man, meaning “battlefield master." I’m getting all this from Wikipedia, by the way.) In this story, after being exiled by King Alfonso, the Cid spends his time conquering and slaying Muslims, bringing glory to his king and to Christendom. In reality, the Cid spent this period fighting for Muslim rulers, and even defeated several Christian forces. Nevertheless, it is quite true that he was a military leader of great skill and success.<br />		<br />This poem displays all of the earmarks of an oral poem: a repetitive structure, formulaic expressions and epithets, “flat” and extraverted characters, a simple and straightforward narrative. It was committed to paper several generations after the Cid’s death; and before being written down, it benefitted from being told and retold at public gatherings. The first two of the three cantars mainly concern the Cid’s heroic deeds, his triumphs in battle over all who opposed him. The third departs from this and tells of the mistreatment of the Cid’s daughters and the resulting negotiations and trial by combat.<br />		<br />Although I admit I found the poem a bit dull, it does provide a fascinating glimpse into medieval Spain. Quite interesting to me was the heavy value placed on winning booty. (I mean “booty” in the pirate, not in the hip-hop sense.) After every one of the Cid’s victories, it is emphasized again and again how much money he gained. He constantly promises his followers riches and finery and wealth; and this martial pursuit of lucre seems to be regarded by all as honorable and right, a fit pursuit for God-fearing men. <br />		<br />Also instructive was the glimpse into the medieval justice system. After the Cid’s two daughters are literally beaten and left for dead by their husbands, the Cid seems more concerned with his damaged honor than by the fact that his daughters were almost savagely murdered. And even though the perpetrators were obviously guilty and in the wrong, a trial by combat is held—during which the Cid doesn’t even fight—to decide the question of honor and shame. To me, the whole proceeding seemed to miss the point, but of course that’s neither here not there.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1574163342369386570" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_667631447" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/667631447" />
    <a title="Joe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9777942-joe"><img alt="Joe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1422026319p2/9777942.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/667631447?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe" href="/user/show/9777942-joe">Joe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780452007901" href="/book/show/2746745-poem-of-cid">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer667631447" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16458647602270088079">From a modern American perspective, The Cid seems an odd choice for a national hero. He was a highly successful battle commander, but half his career was spent as a raider. He lived off the lands of Moors who had done him no wrong and lived under the protection of christian nobles. So in one of <u>The Poem of The Cid</u>'s most dramatic moments, the 'arrogant' Count of Barcelona challenges The Cid regarding the injustice of his plundering ways. The Cid's response is pure practicality; 'I need it for my</span>
  <span id="freeText16458647602270088079" style="display:none">From a modern American perspective, The Cid seems an odd choice for a national hero. He was a highly successful battle commander, but half his career was spent as a raider. He lived off the lands of Moors who had done him no wrong and lived under the protection of christian nobles. So in one of <u>The Poem of The Cid</u>'s most dramatic moments, the 'arrogant' Count of Barcelona challenges The Cid regarding the injustice of his plundering ways. The Cid's response is pure practicality; 'I need it for my men, who share my pauperdom. We keep alive by taking from you and from others. And while it pleases our heavenly Father, we shall continue thus.' <br /><br />Now, the reason for The Cid's pauperdom has nothing to do with the Count of Barcelona or his Moorish protectorates. The Cid is destitute because he has been denounced by his King at the behest of court schemers; schemers jealous of The Cid's honor and battlefield accomplishments. This hardly seams fair for the Cid's battlefield victims, but using the logic of his time and place; it's his only choice. The Cid must feed his loyal soldiers and he will not betray his King, even though his King betrayed him.<br /><br />But for all the Cid's medieval virtues; courage in the face of overwhelming odds, unswerving loyalty, practical and clever decision-making, his life hangs by a thread throughout the story. If he had lost even one of his many battles, he was likely finished since he had no allies to fall back upon. <br /><br />Most of history's great military commanders are great conquerors. Alexander the Great, Genghis Khan, Napoleon, etc, all turned military success into mighty empires. The Cid's record in battle could stand up to any of them, yet his great prize was merely to reacquire his King's esteem; a curious prize to modern eyes.<br /><br /><i>Edited 1-21-2017</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="16458647602270088079" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer7821818768278139787">This book has it all! Swords! Dancing! And beards! All tangled up in an epic "historic" poem. The Cid--a gallantly bearded knight banished by his king for crimes he did not commit--goes smiting and smashing all over Spain, killing Moors like flies and creating a kingdom of his own by the sweat of his beard. And oh what a beard it is! The Beard of The Cid manages to become arguably one of the greatest sidekicks of all time. I myself am growing my own beard in solidarity with the Beard of the Cid.</span>
  <span id="freeText7821818768278139787" style="display:none">This book has it all! Swords! Dancing! And beards! All tangled up in an epic "historic" poem. The Cid--a gallantly bearded knight banished by his king for crimes he did not commit--goes smiting and smashing all over Spain, killing Moors like flies and creating a kingdom of his own by the sweat of his beard. And oh what a beard it is! The Beard of The Cid manages to become arguably one of the greatest sidekicks of all time. I myself am growing my own beard in solidarity with the Beard of the Cid. And, someday I hope to clutch my beard, giving it a dramatic shake, when ere I speak. But, until then I must content myself with brushing it three times daily and allowing plenty of rest and sunshine. But someday! Oh! I shall read the whole of the Song of the Cid aloud, beard in hand, and then I shalt know happiness in full.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1936701138" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1936701138" />
    <a title="Caroline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5261455-caroline"><img alt="Caroline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506384653p2/5261455.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1936701138?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caroline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caroline" href="/user/show/5261455-caroline">Caroline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/34519304-poem-of-the-cid">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5261455-caroline?shelf=philip-ward-500-books">philip-ward-500-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5261455-caroline?shelf=hispanic">hispanic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1936701138" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14180272972831865195">Another ‘well, no wonder it’s a classic’ from my project of reading from Philip Ward’s <i>A Lifetime's Reading: Five Hundred Great Books to be Enjoyed over 50 Years</i>. <br/><br/>But <i>Poem of the Cid</i> can be two different types of experience. I read two translation side by side. I started with Paul Blackburn’s modern verse translation, when I found it at the library book store. I had just read Blackburn’s translations of troubadour poetry and liked them very much, so I thought I’d continue. But then, once in and </span>
  <span id="freeText14180272972831865195" style="display:none">Another ‘well, no wonder it’s a classic’ from my project of reading from Philip Ward’s <i>A Lifetime's Reading: Five Hundred Great Books to be Enjoyed over 50 Years</i>. <br /><br />But <i>Poem of the Cid</i> can be two different types of experience. I read two translation side by side. I started with Paul Blackburn’s modern verse translation, when I found it at the library book store. I had just read Blackburn’s translations of troubadour poetry and liked them very much, so I thought I’d continue. But then, once in and discovering that Blackburn’s version no doubt employed his artistry in bringing the poem forward in time, I found that I wanted to see the original medieval Spanish. Blackburn being of the ‘it won’t help you so we’re not printing it’ school, I plucked the TBR ratty paperback copy of W.S. Merwin’s translation of the same poem from my shelves. It’s got the original en face. <br /><br />Merwin chose to hew more closely to the original. He couldn’t hope to reproduce the exact music of the original if he kept the meaning, so he chose the latter. However, he did stick to the simple declarative style and line length of the poem, with caesurae indicated by spacing. It works well, but leaves more to the reader to imagine. Originally the poem would have been recited by a jongleur, so the rhythm would have been managed by someone who knew the poem intimately. This version preserves the medieval feel of the poem, and is well done.<br /><br />Blackburn chose to imbue his poem with more explicit evocation of the sarcasm, humor, action, and comradely slang. It comes to life, albeit a modern American life. I don’t think he mangles or alters the poem materially, rather he renders it in a way the events would be conveyed by a good storyteller if they happened last week. In his forword he is emphatic that it is meant to be read aloud. Line lengths vary greatly, with enjambment replacing the caesura as a tool. Thus Blackburn’s has an onward momentum that the more regular original makes up for with traditional rhythm.<br /><br />An example:<br /><br />Part One, verse 35<br /><blockquote>
  <i>Enbraçan los ecudoes delante los coraçones<br />abaxan las lanças abueltas de los pendones,<br />enclinaron las caras de suso de los razones,<br />ívanlos ferir de fuertes coraçones.<br />A grandes vozes llama el que en buen ora naçió:<br />“!feridlos, cavaliers, por amor del Criador!<br />Yo so Roy Díaz, el Çid de Bivar Campeador!”<br />Todos fireman en el az do esta Per Vermudoz.<br />Trezientas lanças son, todas tienan pendores;<br />señor moros mataron, todos de señas golpes;<br />a la tornada que fazen otros tantos muertos son.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Merwin:<br /><blockquote>They clasp their shields over their hearts,<br />they lower their lances swathed in their pennons,<br />they bowed their faces over their saddletrees,<br />with strong hearts they charged to attack them.<br />He who in good hour was born cried with a great voice:<br />“Attack them, knights, for the love of the Creator!<br />I am Ruy Diaz, the Çid, the Campeador of Bivar!”<br />All rushed at the rank where Pedro Bermudez was.<br />They were three hundred spears each with its pennon;<br />all struck blows and killed as many Moors;<br />on the second charge they killed three hundred more.</blockquote><br /><br />Blackburn:<br /><blockquote>They bring their shields in front of their chests<br />lower their lances into position flying their pennons, bend<br />their heads low over the fronts of their saddles and<br />charge to the fight, their hearts bursting with courage.<br /><blockquote>In a great voice, he who was born in a good hour cried:<br />“Cut them down, gentlemen, for the Creator’s love! I<br />am Ruy Diaz de Bivar, the Cid Campeador!”</blockquote><br />They charge into the column where Pedro Bermudez is, <br />300<br />lances, each with its pennon,<br /><blockquote>each struck through its mark,<br /><blockquote>each took a Moor with it.</blockquote></blockquote><br />Rode out, turned and charged again,<br />300 m0re were dead.</blockquote><br /><br />(nice ‘Moor’/‘more’ at the end of both translations)(note: Goodreads makes it really clunky to HTML the mid-line caesura in the Spanish and Merwin, so insert it in the logical place).<br /><br />It should be noted here that even though this particular battle is against the Moors, their is no Crusader zeal in evidence. This poem is all about booty. King Alfonso of Leon has thrown the Cid out of his kingdom, and the guy needs a constant stream of income to feed his entourage. It just so happens that all the land to the south, where the Cid can be out of reach, is held by the Moors. The land they travel through is pretty barren, so after every victory they need to move on to find a green pasture. A pasture that happens to belong to the Moors. Thus, many lively battle scenes.<br /><br />Once ownership is settled, he’s perfectly happy to make friends, live and let live. He’s also generous, spreading the captured wealth with the gang, and sending some back to Alfonso in hopes of making peace. Finally they take Valencia, and the Cid figures he’s rich enough. The Cid has Moorish allies, and he doesn't hesitate to attack Christians who offend him. Of course the Moors aren’t happy and the ruler in Morrocco tries twice to retake Valencia. Fifty thousand Moors to four thousand of the Cid’s men: no problem: “only 104 [Moors] escaped". Eventually he gives enough to Alfonso to win forgiveness, but he stays in Valencia; it probably seems safer to keep an eye on the Moors than risk the politics of the Spanish court. And the climate is nice.<br /><br />The second half of the book is quite different, although still, in form, holding to Medieval models. There is a romance thread, great swords, long journeys with carefully articulated routes, and a tournament. But they are just the structure for wonderful character studies of the brave and clever Cid, the incredibly stupid and cowardly braggart aristocratic brothers who wed his daughters, and the enigmatic Alfonso. The highlight is the trial, in which the Cid hornswoggles the brothers and their clan by deceptively feinting his full intentions, step by step.<br /><br />Underlying the whole book, however, is the powerful force of honor. Everyone is quick to take offense, and adamant about defending his honor. This can become highly ridiculous, to us. During the trial scene, for example, the Cid has tied up his long beard so no one can pull it, which would be a great dishonor. Through all my reading in subsequent Spanish literature honor stays foremost, a great waste of energy and a dibilitating preoccupation for a country, it seems to me.<br /><br />So, as far as which translation, I come down on the side of Blackburn. Plus there are his occasional tongue in cheek notes. At one point the Cid’s strategy rests on attacking as the enemy comes downhill, with their cinches loose and riding a different form of saddle than the Cid’s men use. The Cid’s men rout them of course. Blackburn, in the notes at the end: “It was those low cantles on the Catalan horses. Always use a Galician saddle.”<br /><br />And finally a line which I bet has become common in Spanish. The Cid has set up the cowardly brothers and their dastardly uncle; they to have to do combat with three of his trusty knights, over an insult to his family. He entreats his men to fight well (he won’t be at the tournament). One of them replies:<br /><br /><blockquote><i>Dixo Martín Antolínez: “Por qué lo dezides, señor!<br />Preso avemos el debdo e a passar es por nos;<br />podedes odir de muertos çde vencidos no.”</i><br /><br />Martín Antolínez said, “Why do you say this, lord?<br />We have accepted the charge, it is for us to carry it out;<br />you may hear of dead men but not of vanquished.”</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="14180272972831865195" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13162934636099606935">The Song of the Cid, like The Song of Roland, is a story from the Spanish frontier, set amidst the wars between Christians and Moors. The opening of the poem has been lost, but by this chance we are dropped straight into the action. The Cid, already a legendary warrior in the service of King Alfonso of Castile, has been given nine days to leave the kingdom. We find him in tears, leaving his home at Vivar.   <span id="freeText13162934636099606935" style="display:none">The Song of the Cid, like The Song of Roland, is a story from the Spanish frontier, set amidst the wars between Christians and Moors. The opening of the poem has been lost, but by this chance we are dropped straight into the action. The Cid, already a legendary warrior in the service of King Alfonso of Castile, has been given nine days to leave the kingdom. We find him in tears, leaving his home at Vivar. Then it's into the saddle and away into exile in the Moorish borderlands between Toledo and Valencia. Forced to part from his beloved wife and daughters, no money to take with him, a following of only a small band of faithful friends, and no chance of finding safety and rest except in whatever towns he can conquer, the Cid faces annihilation.<br /><br />Still, the legend is not for nothing, and bit by bit the Cid fights his way into wealth and a kingdom, even regaining Alfonso's good graces through his loyalty and generosity. But it's when a pair of unscrupulous fortune-hunters set their eyes on an alliance with his family that the Cid proves his true worth.<br /><br />(Detailed review available now at <a href="http://www.vintagenovels.com/2017/03/the-song-of-cid-trans-burton-raffel.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Vintage Novels!</a>)</span>

<div id="review_1163563686" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1163563686" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2530270-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579281150p2/2530270.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1163563686?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/2530270-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780520250109" href="/book/show/261091.The_Poem_of_the_Cid">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2530270-mike?shelf=books-owned">books-owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1163563686" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4242621553389857736">I've wanted to read The Song of My Cid since the age of 16, when I visited Toledo, Spain, and saw El Cid's (alleged) sword Tizona on display. Our local tour guide told us of Toledo's place in the story, but it was the memory of Tizona that stuck with me, and I told myself that one day I'd read the epic. I can't believe it has taken me sixteen years (literally half my lifetime), but I finally got around to reading it. The experience was well worth the wait. In fact, I'm glad I waited and had a ch</span>
  <span id="freeText4242621553389857736" style="display:none">I've wanted to read The Song of My Cid since the age of 16, when I visited Toledo, Spain, and saw El Cid's (alleged) sword Tizona on display. Our local tour guide told us of Toledo's place in the story, but it was the memory of Tizona that stuck with me, and I told myself that one day I'd read the epic. I can't believe it has taken me sixteen years (literally half my lifetime), but I finally got around to reading it. The experience was well worth the wait. In fact, I'm glad I waited and had a chance to read most of the other major medieval epics just so I could realize how great this one is in comparison. Also, I'm at a point in my life where I'm beginning to take an interest in my Spanish heritage. (My mother's side of the family comes from the Spanish Canary Islands, and before that, the Extremadura region just west of the setting for El Cid.) <br /><br />While the beginning seems like merely a list of battles and riches won, the middle section soon turns into a surprisingly emotional account of a just, unselfish knight who regains his honor. The truly great part of the epic, however, is the final courtroom climax in Toledo. It was surprising to me, given that I hadn't read anything remotely like it in medieval literature. (Granted, my knowledge of medieval lit is limited.) It added a sense of rising tension and realism to the final duel. In those final 30 pages I was as enthralled as if I were reading a modern legal thriller. Ultimately, this epic was a wonderful mix of legend, history, political machinations, and bloody battles. A great read!</span>
  <a data-text-id="4242621553389857736" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_841611469" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/841611469" />
    <a title="Jim Coughenour" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/180643-jim-coughenour"><img alt="Jim Coughenour" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535843128p2/180643.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim Coughenour" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim Coughenour" href="/user/show/180643-jim-coughenour">Jim Coughenour</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780520250109" href="/book/show/261091.The_Poem_of_the_Cid">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/180643-jim-coughenour?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer841611469" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14417226412096119228">A book I never thought I'd read – possibly because I was appalled by <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/El_Cid_%28film%29' rel='nofollow noopener'>Charlton Heston</a> at an impressionable age. Compared to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15953872-the-song-of-roland-translated-by-john-duval?from_search=true' rel='nofollow noopener'>Robocop Roland</a>, El Cid is a complex, amiable guy. The archetypal Spanish hero is clearly the stuff of legend, a champion passionately determined to serve his king by conquering everything in sight, rather than the grand brigand Rodrigo Diaz probably was in bitter truth. (See Richard Fletcher's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/261089.The_Quest_for_El_Cid?from_search=true' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Quest for El Cid</a>). Lesley Byrd Simpson's translation is a quick read, pleasura</span>
  <span id="freeText14417226412096119228" style="display:none">A book I never thought I'd read – possibly because I was appalled by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/El_Cid_%28film%29" rel="nofollow noopener">Charlton Heston</a> at an impressionable age. Compared to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15953872-the-song-of-roland-translated-by-john-duval?from_search=true" rel="nofollow noopener">Robocop Roland</a>, El Cid is a complex, amiable guy. The archetypal Spanish hero is clearly the stuff of legend, a champion passionately determined to serve his king by conquering everything in sight, rather than the grand brigand Rodrigo Diaz probably was in bitter truth. (See Richard Fletcher's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/261089.The_Quest_for_El_Cid?from_search=true" rel="nofollow noopener">The Quest for El Cid</a>). Lesley Byrd Simpson's translation is a quick read, pleasurable in the way <i>Illustrated Classics</i> were for me as a boy.<br /><br />One thing that sets Cid apart from the warriors of Charlemagne is his obvious love for his wife and daughters. When he's forced to leave them behind so he can ravage the Moors, his parting is described in what is perhaps the most vivid image in the book:<blockquote>Ah, such sorrow was never seen! They parted as the nail is parted from the flesh.</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="14417226412096119228" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review841611469">
  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_1464263173" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1464263173" />
    <a title="Miquel Reina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34207574-miquel-reina"><img alt="Miquel Reina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513490698p2/34207574.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1464263173?book_show_action=true">Dec 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Miquel Reina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Miquel Reina" href="/user/show/34207574-miquel-reina">Miquel Reina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789871165254" href="/book/show/73416.El_cantar_del_mio_Cid">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34207574-miquel-reina?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34207574-miquel-reina?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34207574-miquel-reina?shelf=spanish">spanish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1464263173" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16558202007923779012">This is undoubtedly, one of the great classic poems of Spanish literature of medieval chivalry. I read it during my school days but I still remember it as one of the most well designed stories of the medieval society. The poem tells the story of a knight who has been banished and all the stories and adventures that he will force to live for it. It's a good book to understand much better the Middle Ages, their customs and values, entrenched the concept of honor and the importance of recovering it</span>
  <span id="freeText16558202007923779012" style="display:none">This is undoubtedly, one of the great classic poems of Spanish literature of medieval chivalry. I read it during my school days but I still remember it as one of the most well designed stories of the medieval society. The poem tells the story of a knight who has been banished and all the stories and adventures that he will force to live for it. It's a good book to understand much better the Middle Ages, their customs and values, entrenched the concept of honor and the importance of recovering it as one of the most precious things. I recommend it for all fans of the medieval poetry and tales of chivalry.<br /><br />Spanish version:<br />Éste es sin lugar a dudas uno de los grandes poemas clásicos de la literatura Española de caballería medieval. Lo leí como lectura obligatoria en mi época escolar pero aún así lo recuerdo como uno de los relatos más bien trazados de la época. El poema nos cuenta la historia de un caballero que ha sido desterrado y todas las historias y peripecias que deberá vivir por ello. Es un buen libro para entender mejor la época medieval, sus costumbres y sus valores, muy arraigados al concepto de honor y de su recuperación como el bien más preciado. Recomendable para todos aquellos aficionados a la poesía medieval y a los relatos de caballería. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16558202007923779012" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_846598097" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/846598097" />
    <a title="Chris Duval" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26679722-chris-duval"><img alt="Chris Duval" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1387818852p2/26679722.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/846598097?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Duval" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Duval" href="/user/show/26679722-chris-duval">Chris Duval</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143105657" href="/book/show/5920997-the-song-of-the-cid">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26679722-chris-duval?shelf=lit-221-1259ce-europe">lit-221-1259ce-europe</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer846598097" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3382017610428112048">The first half of this tale speaks of the successful marauding of the exile, El Cid, who attacked one civilized place after another. It reminded me of a passage in Gibbon about the Normans: “his Norman followers, excluded from their native and their promised land, wandered among the hills and valleys of Italy, and earned their daily subsistence by the sword.” (Chapter LVI of the <i>Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire.</i>) Later on, the (ahistorical) mal-treatment of the hero’s daughters reminded me o</span>
  <span id="freeText3382017610428112048" style="display:none">The first half of this tale speaks of the successful marauding of the exile, El Cid, who attacked one civilized place after another. It reminded me of a passage in Gibbon about the Normans: “his Norman followers, excluded from their native and their promised land, wandered among the hills and valleys of Italy, and earned their daily subsistence by the sword.” (Chapter LVI of the <i>Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire.</i>) Later on, the (ahistorical) mal-treatment of the hero’s daughters reminded me of the brutality related in the George R. R. Martin <i>Fire and Ice books</i>.<br /><br />This medieval barbarism is relayed in fluid, translated English courtesy of Burton Raffel. My only gripe, an idiosyncratic one, is that he modernized the place names (and a few terms). Thus “…Mio Cid el de Bivar/ e passó a Alfama, la Foz ayuso va,/ passó a Bovierca e a Teca…” becomes “my Cid, the Warrior from Vivar, moved on,/ Passing Alhama, then La Hoz,/ Then Bubieca and even Ateca,…” (I.26). And “un moro Latinado” becomes “a Moor who understood Spanish,” (III.126) a choice that hides the acknowledged linguistic debt to Roma. This connection to the past, brought out in a footnote, resembles how today the various mutually unintelligible languages of the Maghreb, Mesopotamia, the north Levant, Egypt and Arabia all say they speak Arabic.<br /><br />The introduction by María Rosa Menocal explores the origins of the text. Interestingly there is quite the dispute as to whether it arose from oral tradition, or from high culture. The scholar Ramón Menéndez Pidal had insisted on the former, and his opinion was embraced by Franco’s Nationalists. This fascist bias towards the unwritten, and thus towards the pure (in their minds) starts for national treasures was elucidated well in another context by Martin Bernal, in his <i>Black Athena,</i> Vol. I. Nazi theory held the Greeks to be the child-race from which Aryans sprung, and thus it was important to them that Homer was uninfluenced by the nearby literate Afro-Asiatic cultures.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3382017610428112048" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_231562729" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/231562729" />
    <a title="Walt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/762314-walt"><img alt="Walt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1263499503p2/762314.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/231562729?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Walt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Walt" href="/user/show/762314-walt">Walt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780520250109" href="/book/show/261091.The_Poem_of_the_Cid">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/762314-walt?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/762314-walt?shelf=history-medieval">history-medieval</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer231562729" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13271668122943241916">This book would have benefited from a greater analysis of the historical characters, rather than some cursory observations about the historiography. King Alfonso is generally remembered as a conniving treacherous lord and the Cid was something of a rogue warrior often at odds with this kingdom. Some of this is evident in the story as the banished Cid (no clear reason why "good Alfonso" banished him) wonders across Spain raiding and fighting Christians and Muslims, Spaniards and Catalans. His onl</span>
  <span id="freeText13271668122943241916" style="display:none">This book would have benefited from a greater analysis of the historical characters, rather than some cursory observations about the historiography. King Alfonso is generally remembered as a conniving treacherous lord and the Cid was something of a rogue warrior often at odds with this kingdom. Some of this is evident in the story as the banished Cid (no clear reason why "good Alfonso" banished him) wonders across Spain raiding and fighting Christians and Muslims, Spaniards and Catalans. His only quest appears to be warfare. That makes him little better than a drunken soccer hooligan. <br /><br />Full of warfare and glory that is difficult to believe, there are some interesting observations. There is a great amount of fighting between cross and crescent, but there is no discussion of politics, religion, economics, or society. It is just something Spaniards do. The crux of the story was the weakness of the princes of Carrion, Christians. Consequently, the poem itself seems to showcase societal rivalry between the nobility and the lower classes. There may be some geopolitical rivalry too as the Cid was from Castille, and the princes were from Leon. The role of women is also shown in stark reality as property, not individual human beings. It is certainly strange to people in 21st Century America reading that the Cid appears to be more outraged that his sons-in-law had kept his prized swords - which he gave to them as presents - than he was in their treatment of his daughters. Weird. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13271668122943241916" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_618448935" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/618448935" />
    <a title="Erika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4532400-erika"><img alt="Erika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523323123p2/4532400.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/618448935?book_show_action=true">May 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erika" href="/user/show/4532400-erika">Erika</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789700757528" href="/book/show/17932338-poema-de-mio-cid">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer618448935" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13725040921004340461">Oh, mio Cid! I'm glad I wasn't forced to read this when I was a teen 'cause I'm sure I would have hated it. Now that I'm almost on my mid twenties I decided to give it a shot; I expected it to be boring and long and I was surprised to have be it be simple but emotive and even interesting. Perhaps it's just me being a fan of knights of any kind but I enjoyed it. <br/>The language wasn't too archaic to be tiring, or at least it was not in comparison to the Greek texts I've been reading lately. Things I</span>
  <span id="freeText13725040921004340461" style="display:none">Oh, mio Cid! I'm glad I wasn't forced to read this when I was a teen 'cause I'm sure I would have hated it. Now that I'm almost on my mid twenties I decided to give it a shot; I expected it to be boring and long and I was surprised to have be it be simple but emotive and even interesting. Perhaps it's just me being a fan of knights of any kind but I enjoyed it. <br />The language wasn't too archaic to be tiring, or at least it was not in comparison to the Greek texts I've been reading lately. Things I didn't like was the constant naming of places and people that didn't really add much to the text. I read on one of the footnotes of the book that some of the locations mentioned were very accurate with Spain's geography, which is nice and would have been appreciated by me if I was a Spaniard.<br />One thing I was a bit bothered by was the fact that Cid didn't come across as really clever. Most of the battle strategies were someone else's, usually coming from cousin Minaya Alvar Fáñez. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ He was also easily tricked by the Carrión brothers and when he realized he was affronted by them he just asked to have his money and weapons back and cajoled others to get revenge on the offenders, all this while he headed back home to chill on his mansion.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> It seemed to me like he went on taking other people's credit all the time. <br />Bottom line, it wasn't so bad and it went by fast enough for me to be forgiving of its downsides.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13725040921004340461" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17846174321703974695">The Song of Cid takes you to medieval times and stories of brave knights, from which Rodrigo Diaz di Bivar, or El Cid, was the bravest. A book from early 14th century, telling tales about a Spanish vassal, fighting against both extreme odds and various enemies, Moors and Christians alike. <br/><br/>What I liked about the book was the look back on the medieval times and the knightly mentality. Not only is Cid a pious Christian, but a loyal vassal to a king from whose service he was banished, serving him ne</span>
  <span id="freeText17846174321703974695" style="display:none">The Song of Cid takes you to medieval times and stories of brave knights, from which Rodrigo Diaz di Bivar, or El Cid, was the bravest. A book from early 14th century, telling tales about a Spanish vassal, fighting against both extreme odds and various enemies, Moors and Christians alike. <br /><br />What I liked about the book was the look back on the medieval times and the knightly mentality. Not only is Cid a pious Christian, but a loyal vassal to a king from whose service he was banished, serving him nevertheless. This is also a book of many furious battles, a marriage, and a betrayal properly punished. Some of the scenes in the book make you smile (50 000 Moors vs 3000 Christians - guess who wins), and some get you even more drawn inside the story. The book is read in one breath.<br /><br />****</span>

<div id="review_3515441409" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3515441409" />
    <a title="martin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16675-martin"><img alt="martin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1185961568p2/16675.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3515441409?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="martin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="martin" href="/user/show/16675-martin">martin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780520011762" href="/book/show/452471.The_Poem_of_the_Cid">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16675-martin?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16675-martin?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16675-martin?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3515441409" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11679171096981547542">This prose translation by Lesley Byrd Simpson obviously lacks the beauty and difficulty of the original mediaeval Spanish poem, but in return makes the tale more accessible to modern readers while still retaining its liveliness and positivity. <br/><br/>As with much other vernacular European literature of that period, the unknown author is not just telling a thrilling tale or entertaining his audience, but is also educating his readers and encouraging them to follow the Cid’s example of loyalty, honesty, </span>
  <span id="freeText11679171096981547542" style="display:none">This prose translation by Lesley Byrd Simpson obviously lacks the beauty and difficulty of the original mediaeval Spanish poem, but in return makes the tale more accessible to modern readers while still retaining its liveliness and positivity. <br /><br />As with much other vernacular European literature of that period, the unknown author is not just telling a thrilling tale or entertaining his audience, but is also educating his readers and encouraging them to follow the Cid’s example of loyalty, honesty, piety and courtly virtues (the defrauding of the Jewish moneylenders notwithstanding). In all his exploits, the Cid is seen as a fair and generous man and a wise and just leader. It is interesting that he is also merciful to his enemies on the battlefield, killing only when necessary. His treatment of cooperative Moors in conquered cities and his friendship with the good Moor Abengalbon show that while the story proclaims the superiority of Christianity, it is not here the exclusive, vindictive religion of the later Inquisition. <br /><br />Yes, it’s a simple story of good guys and bad guys - and the bad guys are gloriously, irretrievably bad - but if we go beyond that to see the ideal the writer is promoting to his readers, it also tells us a lot about the society and morality of the time. Typically if the writer is telling us to behave like the Cid, he feels we are not currently living up to that ideal! </span>
  <a data-text-id="11679171096981547542" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3448428127" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3448428127" />
    <a title="Galicius" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3875148-galicius"><img alt="Galicius" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1562633428p2/3875148.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3448428127?book_show_action=true">Jul 17, 2020</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer10478773375244092978">“The Poem of My Cid is probably the earliest monument of Spanish literature. <br/><br/>“Rodrigo Diaz, the Cid . . . Rodrigo returned, and appealing in turn to ruse and force, at last sat down before the city to reduce it by famine. During the last period of the siege, those who fled from the city to escape the famine were thrown to dogs, or burned at slow fires. The city capitulated on favorable terms, June 15th, 1094. But all the conditions of the capitulation were violated. The Cadi-President was buried</span>
  <span id="freeText10478773375244092978" style="display:none">“The Poem of My Cid is probably the earliest monument of Spanish literature. <br /><br />“Rodrigo Diaz, the Cid . . . Rodrigo returned, and appealing in turn to ruse and force, at last sat down before the city to reduce it by famine. During the last period of the siege, those who fled from the city to escape the famine were thrown to dogs, or burned at slow fires. The city capitulated on favorable terms, June 15th, 1094. But all the conditions of the capitulation were violated. The Cadi-President was buried in a trench up to his arm-pits, surrounded with burning brands, and slowly tortured to death, several of his kinsmen and friends sharing his fate. Rodrigo was with difficulty restrained from throwing into the flames the Cadi's children and the women of his harem. Yet the lives and property of Ibn Djahhof and his family had been expressly safeguarded in the capitulation. It is probable that Rodrigo's title of "the Cid" or "my Cid" (Arabic, Sid-y = my lord) was given to him at this time by his Moorish subjects.”<br /><br />“At last a formidable coalition is formed against Spain. The Emperor of Germany and the King of France, supported by the Pope and Patriarch require Spain, in recognition of her feudal dependence upon the Roman Empire, a yearly tribute of fifteen noble virgins, besides silver, horses, falcons, etc<br /><br />From “The Cid “1045?-1099) Introduction by Charles Sprague Smith</span>

<div id="review_2584997494" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2584997494" />
    <a title="Kathryn Wilmotte" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16249298-kathryn-wilmotte"><img alt="Kathryn Wilmotte" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589045294p2/16249298.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2584997494?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathryn Wilmotte" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathryn Wilmotte" href="/user/show/16249298-kathryn-wilmotte">Kathryn Wilmotte</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140444469" href="/book/show/261088.The_Poem_of_the_Cid">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2584997494" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17001038172466903923">“What a good vassal. If only he had a good lord!”<br/><br/>Because I teach both texts in our Medieval Voices course, <i>The Poem of the Cid</i> will forever be linked in my mind with <i>The Song of Roland</i>. There are similarities: questions of honor, of what makes a good vassal, of Christian heroes inhabiting a predominantly Muslim world. But there are vast differences as well--for one, the Cid is not on a quest to convert or kill all Muslims, as Charlemagne and Roland are in <i>SoR</i>. In fact, the Cid seems remarkably u</span>
  <span id="freeText17001038172466903923" style="display:none">“What a good vassal. If only he had a good lord!”<br /><br />Because I teach both texts in our Medieval Voices course, <i>The Poem of the Cid</i> will forever be linked in my mind with <i>The Song of Roland</i>. There are similarities: questions of honor, of what makes a good vassal, of Christian heroes inhabiting a predominantly Muslim world. But there are vast differences as well--for one, the Cid is not on a quest to convert or kill all Muslims, as Charlemagne and Roland are in <i>SoR</i>. In fact, the Cid seems remarkably uninterested in the religious beliefs of others and is even good friends with the Muslim leader Abengalbón.<br /><br />The Cid is on a quest to restore his personal fortune, not defend his faith, and in this he is remarkably successful (the author seems to run out of ways to describe just how much wealth the Cid acquires). Perhaps this is what is most different from <i>The Song of Roland</i>--the tone. The Cid is relentlessly optimistic; every battle is just an exciting chance to gain more booty. We have none of Charlemagne’s sighing, no elegiac laments. The Cid takes everything in stride and forces things to turn out in his favor. He’s sensible, pragmatic, easy-going, but also calculating and clever. <br /><br />But while the Cid wins all his battles (literal and figurative), he still ends up wounded where it will hurt most: the humiliation and pain of his daughters (whom he genuinely loves) by their husbands. This family drama covers about half the poem and is the part I find most fascinating.<br /><br />Because the Cid must prove himself to be a loyal vassal to King Alfonso, he cannot do anything to jeopardize a relationship so recently restored. And so he cannot protest the marriage of his daughters to the Infantes of Carrión, despite his considerable reservations. I imagine that the years the Infantes spend married to the Cid’s daughters as years of uneasy coexistence at best. The Cid, ever the optimist, tries to find something good in his disappointing sons-in-law. His daughters, bound by obedience to husbands and father, cannot say what they need to say, what their husbands are really like. When their husbands decide to take them to Carrión, they can only beg their father to send them news of him; I read this interchange as a silent, desperate cry for help. <br /><br />The shaming of the Cid’s daughters (and their subsequent triumph as Queens of Navarre and Aragon) is (to me) the centerpiece of the poem, contrasting true honor with false, the noble Cid against the delusional and arrogant Infantes.<br /><br />It is this set of contrasts and the unrelenting cheerfulness and quick wits of the Cid that make this such a fascinating poem, especially when read in conjunction with <i>The Song of Roland</i>.<br /><br />5 stars. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17001038172466903923" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1041481328" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1041481328" />
    <a title="Charles Dee Mitchell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2272557-charles-dee-mitchell"><img alt="Charles Dee Mitchell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267455462p2/2272557.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1041481328?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charles Dee Mitchell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charles Dee Mitchell" href="/user/show/2272557-charles-dee-mitchell">Charles Dee Mitchell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143105657" href="/book/show/5920997-the-song-of-the-cid">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2272557-charles-dee-mitchell?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1041481328" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13869571339135979792">Since I had recently read <i>The Song of Roland</i>, I thought I would give its Spanish counterpart a try. Although they were written around the same time, the French author was dealing with events 500 years in the past and didn't hesitate to fantasize. (At one point the angel Gabriel stops the sun in the sky to give Charlemagne's troops time to catch up with the fleeing Saracens.) By comparison, this account of the life of Rodrigo Dias, El Cid, comes off as relatively realistic. But when establishing </span>
  <span id="freeText13869571339135979792" style="display:none">Since I had recently read <i>The Song of Roland</i>, I thought I would give its Spanish counterpart a try. Although they were written around the same time, the French author was dealing with events 500 years in the past and didn't hesitate to fantasize. (At one point the angel Gabriel stops the sun in the sky to give Charlemagne's troops time to catch up with the fleeing Saracens.) By comparison, this account of the life of Rodrigo Dias, El Cid, comes off as relatively realistic. But when establishing a figure as a national hero, liberties will be taken.<br /><br />The El Cid narrative is spread across many years, starting with Cid's exile from the court of Alphonso. His small band of followers take town after town away from both Moorish and Christian rulers. His fame grows, he makes friends and enemies, there are many battles. In only a single incident does a blow from Cid's sword cleave a man in half from his skull through his pelvis. In <i>Roland</i> this is a common battlefield maneuver. But the author of this epic saves the effect for Cid's triumph over the King of Morocco. <br /><br />The most enjoyable aspect of this epic is the marriage of Cid's daughters to the high-born Carrion brothers. (If I knew how to add proper accents their name would not look so much like the word for dead meat.) This cowardly pair is only in it for the money, which they make a ton of without ever entering the fight. They decide the best course of action will be to take their brides back to the Carrion estates, but along the way dispose of them. Once they are alone with their wives in the forest, they beat them, strip them, and leave them for dead, assuming wild animals will take care of the evidence. But the daughters are saved and a trial is held to determine the brother's guilt. In a defense that says much about the society of the day, they defend themselves by claiming that their superior social position exempts them from punishment for attempting to do away with such low born trash. This is taken as a serious defense. But things end the way they should.<br /><br />The Carrion brothers are the liveliest element of the epic, an they are also one element that is a completely fictional creation.<br /><br />With <i>The Song of the Cid</i> I am calling it quits on Medieval Epics.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13869571339135979792" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1041481328">

<div id="review_1704715282" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1704715282" />
    <a title="Raymond" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17968808-raymond"><img alt="Raymond" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1370201564p2/17968808.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1704715282?book_show_action=true">Jul 21, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Raymond" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Raymond" href="/user/show/17968808-raymond">Raymond</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143105657" href="/book/show/5920997-the-song-of-the-cid">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1704715282" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1807756569265169186">I previously rated the Song of Roland at five stars; now I'm thinking I should've given that four stars, because this one raises the bar as far as chansons de geste go.<br/><br/>The Song of the Cid or El Cantar de Mio Cid tells the story of Rodrigo Diaz de Vivar, an 11th century Castilian knight who is sent in exile by his king and works to regain his favour by making war on the Moors - and that's about as much as you can say without spoiling the plot, as this is not a long book. It reads very similarly t</span>
  <span id="freeText1807756569265169186" style="display:none">I previously rated the Song of Roland at five stars; now I'm thinking I should've given that four stars, because this one raises the bar as far as chansons de geste go.<br /><br />The Song of the Cid or El Cantar de Mio Cid tells the story of Rodrigo Diaz de Vivar, an 11th century Castilian knight who is sent in exile by his king and works to regain his favour by making war on the Moors - and that's about as much as you can say without spoiling the plot, as this is not a long book. It reads very similarly to the Song of Roland, but the story is slightly more complex and the author's knowledge of Iberian society (the Moors in particular) is far better, so that it reads more like a romanticized version of historical events than a pure fantasy, although the central conflict hinges on a gruesome incident that is almost certainly fictional and by modern standards the plot is still very predictable. Speaking as a medievalist this is a great window into the mentality of the knightly class and the political situation in Iberia in the 12th century, though I suspect a reader who's only interested in a good story will find less to enjoy.<br /><br />Finally I should note that no complete manuscript of this story survives, and it misses the beginning as well as a few lines here and there. These parts can be guessed at based on other, historical sources about El Cid, and as far as possible the translator has included summaries of their events. Regardless I do not deduct a star as this is how The Song of the Cid has been read for centuries, much like Petrarch's Satyricon, and the incompleteness is simply a part of the work's identity at this point.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1807756569265169186" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
